Putting aside the obvious "Old man yells at cloud" aspect of this, 1 google search instantly show there were at *least* 9 songs titled "Money" released by 1970. There are countless others, but I think the point has been made. Songs about acquiring, desiring, or flaunting wealth are *far* from a new phenomenon.

This is an expected outcome of the cultural and governmental policy shift that began with Reagan in the 80's. By creating the conditions for unbridled wealth to accumulate (as it has in the past) it causes the culture to see wealth as the main arbiter of value. It becomes the goal of life and that's what we are now seeing. Cultural shifts take decades to play out and will continue along a path until there is a break. The Great Depression created such a break in the previous cycle and we went from the 1930's to 1980 with a changed cultural perspective. It's easy to make a point when you pick cherries, as Bill so often does. What about Eartha Kitt's "Just an Old Fashioned Girl" and "Santa Baby"? What about "We're in the Money" and so many other songs featured in the Busby Berkley "Gold Diggers" movies?  Fun fact: music used to get  its wider audience via the radio. The choices were curated by station management or the DJs, sometimes with a payola assist. Spotify and other streaming sources let every user create their own playlist from a nearly infinite catalog by comparison. As a result, the corollary to Sturgeon's law comes into effect. (Sturgeon's Law: 99% of SF is crap. The corollary: 99% of everything is crap.) It may be harder to find older songs that fit the mold, but that's only because  they've been filtered out by initial availability and by time.  The biggest difference between the generations is that "wine, women, and song" became "sex, drugs, and rock 'n' roll". I'm not hip enough to know the current mantra.

Let's not forget that our brains evolved to prioritize short term rewards and hoard resources. What helped us survive and then thrive as a species, those very characteristics will most likely lead us to be unable to sacrifice short term gains and tackle long term challenges. Don't believe me? Ask yourself how many people you know that responded to the rising costs of groceries in recent years by planting a garden and growing their own produce. Just because our species has had success in the past and possesses the ability to use higher reasoning like logic and planning, doesn't mean that continued success is guaranteed or that we will be able to overcome our older, simpler limbic system anytime soon. Consider how easily emotions can lead to a lack of progress in governance, when the logical thing to do is compromise. Hoarding resources wasn't a problem when there were fewer humans and therefore more available resources.  While technological innovation has led to a faster extraction and utilization of resources (and resulted in rapid population expansion), control of those resources will always be in the hands of the few at the expense of the many. Access to them will never be equal. There's wars going on in the world right now over resources that appear to be based in political or religious ideological differences.... but there is a reason to invade another country if doing so gives you the opportunity to control almost all of the global supply of a widely consumed grain. Controlling that resource alone allows you control and influence over the countries who rely on that resource, which in turn will provide greater access to the resources of those countries which may be metals, gemstones, oil, soldiers, anything highly valuable that can lead to future success in other wars. In the near future, groundwater supplies will become a larger driver of global conflict than it already is, particularly in arid countries. Keep in mind, many of these conflicts come down to disagreement on whose ancestral lineage has a better claim over the best land (it's all about those resources), and whose future lineage will have access to and control of the best land. The fact that these populations are genetic cousins with a shared ancestral lineage in the same land has become irrelevant, there is simply not enough resources for both cultures to thrive and also ensure the success of future generations. Religious differences merely provide a more palatable justification of the killing of one's enemies, and is an effective tool to motivate a populace to engage in war and always has been, and thus will sadly continue to be the reason why most believe they are at war with their neighbors.  All this is to say, perhaps the children of today who grow up to value and pursue economic resources will have an evolutionary advantage in a world where the earth will have far exceeded its carrying capacity and resources will have become much more scarce than today's world or the world of the recent past. While they may appear to be different values than those of older generations, it seems presumptuous to decide that a greater focus on hoarding economic resources will be an undesirable trait to pass on to future generations at this point in human history. Granted, no one knows how many more of those future generations will survive a rapidly changing climate. Even best estimates don't paint an easy picture for many of us to comprehend and act on collectively. As individuals who are all interested in passing on our genetics, logic dictates that we should be acting cooperatively toward that shared long-term goal. But that's unlikely to happen any time soon, as it would require us to sacrifice our short term gains, which we are particularly bad at doing. We're currently busy trying to figure out if democracy is even something that can be sustained long-term in any part of the world. It's not been that long since we as a species began to topple kingdoms, and even less time since religious institutions began to lose control over some individuals in their communities and corporations began to increase their power over everyone.  These neo-cortices of ours are still too new. Too new for so many things, including for us to responsibly use the technology we've invented, let alone understand the future implications of much of it. This is evident in the degrading mental health of young people being linked so strongly to social media use, and that's just one example of recent studies pointing to an error in judgement in the use of fairly modern technologies. E-cigarettes for vaping is a physical health example, and so is synthetic stone with many of the workers contracting silicosis. You'd have thought we'd have learned our lesson with asbestos. You can probably name more, there's just too many examples and we're only now realizing how plastics can accumulate in our bodies (and brains) while we wait for the ozone to finish recovering from yet another long-term error we made in the pursuit technological advance. Music is more of a reflection on the values of a time than the generator of those values anyway.

Parents can be "in on it" too.  I'm a late Boomer (Born 1960) & altho my Silent Gen blue collar devoutly Catholic working class parents were thrifty & strict on me, they did Aspire to the American Dream.  So, we moved from Row House Philly to a house in the 'burbs in 1969, & I enrolled in 4th grade w/all the kids of wealthy WASP & Jewish doctors, lawyers, business people, professionals, etc.  'Twas quite the culture shock for me, seeing all these kids who had their own TV in their bedroom, stereos, records, a mini bike, Converse All Stars (they were the Bling sneaker of the day).  I had second hand everything from my older cousins, & knew my dad would pin my ears back if I ever asked for those sorts of material goods.  It's no wonder so many grew up not caring about money, as they always had it (great quote from the movie The Aviator in the "Lunch with the Hepburns" scene btw).  Fast forward to nowadays, I have some in-law nieces & nephews on the Gen X/Millennial line who grew up poor, much like my parents, but worked hard to earn a decent suburban lifestyle.  They now spoil & overwhelm their kids with material goods, encourage the whole Bling culture, let their pre teen daughters spend gobs of money on designer makeup & hair treatments, not even understanding how to use such stuff.  Sad.  I guess I don't really have a point, just rambling.  I made a good living for myself as a civil engineer, I'm still pretty thrifty, never bought a new vehicle in my life, paid off my house, have a 401k, don't go on extravagant vacations or gamble at casinos.  The simple pleasures are the best.

There still some good music out there, but it's almost like we've reached the end of the plot arc.  The music of the 20th century rolled along steadily evolving and changing with the rapidly evolving Zeitgeist.  Somewhere around the turn of the millennium, we reached the end of history and all the music reached a saturation point where it ran out of fresh ideas and innovations.  Now it's all just a swirling cacophony of recycled rhythms, with a few stalwarts keeping their respective dying traditions alive.  Like everything in our world, it's become a boundless miasma of noise, noise, noise, wherein even the good stuff that's left gets drowned out by all the competing voices who all sound the same, who all follow the same clich√©s, all of it done to death.
